â”‚   Small enough to rewrite in 2-4 weeks                          â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚

â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”

â”‚   TIP 1: START WITH A MONOLITH                                  â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                  â”‚
â”‚   "Monolith First" approach                                     â”‚
â”‚   Extract services when boundaries become clear                 â”‚
â”‚   Premature decomposition is worse than a monolith              â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 2: DEFINE SERVICE BOUNDARIES CAREFULLY                    â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                    â”‚
â”‚   Use Domain-Driven Design bounded contexts                     â”‚
â”‚   Services should be loosely coupled, highly cohesive           â”‚
â”‚   Wrong boundaries = distributed monolith                       â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 3: DATABASE PER SERVICE                                   â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                      â”‚

â”‚   No shared databases                                           â”‚
â”‚   Use events for data synchronization                           â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 4: EMBRACE EVENTUAL CONSISTENCY                           â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                           â”‚
â”‚   Distributed transactions are hard                             â”‚
â”‚   Design for eventual consistency                               â”‚
â”‚   Use Saga pattern for multi-service operations                 â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚

â”‚                                                                 â”‚

â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                      â”‚

â”‚                                                                 â”‚

â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                    â”‚

â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                      â”‚

â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”

â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 1: MINIMIZE COLD STARTS                                   â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                   â”‚
â”‚   Keep functions small (smaller packages load faster)           â”‚
â”‚   Use provisioned concurrency for latency-sensitive             â”‚
â”‚   Keep functions warm with scheduled pings (use wisely)         â”‚
â”‚   Choose runtimes with faster cold starts                       â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 2: RIGHT-SIZE MEMORY                                      â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                      â”‚
â”‚   Memory affects CPU allocation                                 â”‚
â”‚   More memory = faster execution = might be cheaper             â”‚
â”‚   Profile and find optimal memory setting                       â”‚
â”‚   AWS Lambda Power Tuning tool helps                            â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 3: DESIGN FOR STATELESSNESS                               â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                               â”‚
â”‚   No local state between invocations                            â”‚
â”‚   Use external stores (DynamoDB, Redis, S3)                     â”‚
â”‚   Assume containers are ephemeral                               â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 4: OPTIMIZE DEPENDENCIES                                  â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                  â”‚
â”‚   Only include what you need                                    â”‚
â”‚   Use Lambda layers for shared dependencies                     â”‚
â”‚   Tree-shake unused code                                        â”‚
â”‚   Consider compiled languages for smaller packages              â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 5: HANDLE ERRORS GRACEFULLY                               â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                               â”‚
â”‚   Implement retry logic                                         â”‚
â”‚   Use dead letter queues                                        â”‚
â”‚   Idempotent functions (same input = same result)               â”‚
â”‚   Structured error handling                                     â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 6: USE ASYNC WHERE POSSIBLE                               â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                 â”‚
â”‚   Async invocations are cheaper                                 â”‚
â”‚   Use queues to decouple                                        â”‚
â”‚   Event-driven over request-response                            â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 7: MONITOR AND TRACE                                      â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                      â”‚
â”‚   Distributed tracing (X-Ray, etc.)                             â”‚
â”‚   Custom metrics for business logic                             â”‚
â”‚   Log correlation IDs                                           â”‚
â”‚   Set up alerts for errors and throttling                       â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 8: AVOID FUNCTION SPRAWL                                  â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                  â”‚
â”‚   Don't create too many tiny functions                          â”‚
â”‚   Group related functionality                                   â”‚
â”‚   Use routing within functions where sensible                   â”‚
â”‚   Balance granularity with manageability                        â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 9: PLAN FOR VENDOR LOCK-IN                                â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                â”‚
â”‚   Abstract cloud-specific code                                  â”‚
â”‚   Use frameworks like Serverless Framework, SAM                 â”‚
â”‚   Consider portable options (Knative)                           â”‚
â”‚   Document dependencies on cloud services                       â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚

â”‚   Processing same event twice = same result                     â”‚
â”‚   Use event IDs to detect duplicates                            â”‚
â”‚   Design for "at-least-once" delivery                           â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 3: HANDLE ORDERING CAREFULLY                              â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                              â”‚
â”‚   Events may arrive out of order                                â”‚
â”‚   Use partition keys for related events                         â”‚
â”‚   Include timestamps and sequence numbers                       â”‚
â”‚   Design for eventual consistency                               â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 4: VERSION YOUR EVENTS                                    â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                    â”‚
â”‚   Events evolve over time                                       â”‚
â”‚   Support backward compatibility                                â”‚
â”‚   Use schema evolution strategies                               â”‚
â”‚   Consider event upcasting                                      â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 5: IMPLEMENT DEAD LETTER QUEUES                           â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                           â”‚
â”‚   Failed events need somewhere to go                            â”‚
â”‚   Monitor and alert on DLQ                                      â”‚
â”‚   Have a strategy for reprocessing                              â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 6: USE CORRELATION IDS                                    â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                    â”‚
â”‚   Track events across services                                  â”‚
â”‚   Include correlation ID in all related events                  â”‚
â”‚   Essential for debugging and tracing                           â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 7: CHOOSE RIGHT DELIVERY GUARANTEE                        â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                        â”‚
â”‚   At-most-once: May lose events (rarely acceptable)             â”‚
â”‚   At-least-once: May duplicate (handle with idempotency)        â”‚
â”‚   Exactly-once: Hard to achieve, often not necessary            â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 8: CONSIDER EVENT REPLAY                                  â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                  â”‚
â”‚   Ability to rebuild state from events                          â”‚
â”‚   Useful for new consumers, debugging, recovery                 â”‚
â”‚   Event streaming enables this naturally                        â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 9: BALANCE EVENT SIZE                                     â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                  â”‚
â”‚   Too small: Many roundtrips to get data                        â”‚
â”‚   Too large: Bandwidth, storage costs                           â”‚
â”‚   Include what consumers typically need                         â”‚
â”‚   Consider event-carried state vs notification                  â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜

3.9 Hexagonal Architecture (Ports & Adapters)

Pattern Overview

â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚              HEXAGONAL ARCHITECTURE                              â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   ALSO KNOWN AS:                                                â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ Ports and Adapters                                          â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ Onion Architecture (similar concept)                        â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   ORIGIN: Alistair Cockburn, 2005                               â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   DEFINITION:                                                   â”‚
â”‚   An architectural pattern that allows an application to        â”‚
â”‚   be equally driven by users, programs, automated tests,        â”‚
â”‚   or batch scripts, and to be developed and tested in           â”‚
â”‚   isolation from its eventual runtime devices and databases     â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   CORE IDEA:                                                    â”‚
â”‚   Isolate the application's core logic from external concerns   â”‚
â”‚   using ports (interfaces) and adapters (implementations)       â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   STRUCTURE:                                                    â”‚

â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚              PORTS AND ADAPTERS EXPLAINED                        â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤

â”‚   PORTS (Interfaces):                                           â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                          â”‚
â”‚   Define HOW the application wants to communicate               â”‚
â”‚   Technology-agnostic contracts                                 â”‚
â”‚   Owned by the application core                                 â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   DRIVING PORTS (Primary/Inbound):                              â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                               â”‚

â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”

Hexagonal Architecture Optimization Tips

â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”

â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 1: KEEP THE CORE PURE                                     â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                     â”‚
â”‚   No framework dependencies in domain                           â”‚
â”‚   No infrastructure concerns in business logic                  â”‚
â”‚   Pure business rules and entities                              â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 2: DESIGN PORTS AROUND USE CASES                          â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                          â”‚
â”‚   Ports should reflect business operations                      â”‚
â”‚   Not technical operations                                      â”‚
â”‚   "PlaceOrder" not "InsertOrderRecord"                          â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 3: ADAPTERS ARE THIN                                      â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                      â”‚
â”‚   Adapters only translate                                       â”‚
â”‚   No business logic in adapters                                 â”‚
â”‚   Keep them simple and focused                                  â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 4: TEST AT MULTIPLE LEVELS                                â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                â”‚
â”‚   Unit tests: Core logic with mock ports                        â”‚
â”‚   Integration tests: Real adapters                              â”‚
â”‚   E2E tests: Full system                                        â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 5: ONE ADAPTER PER EXTERNAL SYSTEM                        â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                        â”‚
â”‚   Clear responsibility                                          â”‚
â”‚   Easy to replace                                               â”‚
â”‚   Changes isolated to one place                                 â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 6: USE DEPENDENCY INJECTION                               â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                 â”‚
â”‚   Wire adapters to ports at composition root                    â”‚
â”‚   Core doesn't create its dependencies                          â”‚
â”‚   Enables easy swapping and testing                             â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 7: DON'T OVER-ENGINEER                                    â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                     â”‚
â”‚   Not every small app needs full hexagonal                      â”‚
â”‚   Start simple, extract ports when needed                       â”‚
â”‚   Value comes with actual multiple adapters                     â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 8: HANDLE CROSS-CUTTING CONCERNS                          â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                          â”‚
â”‚   Logging, security at adapter level                            â”‚
â”‚   Or use decorator pattern around ports                         â”‚
â”‚   Keep core focused on business                                 â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜

â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤

â”‚   TIP 1: ENFORCE DEPENDENCY RULE                                â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                â”‚
â”‚   Use architectural tests to verify                             â”‚
â”‚   Inner layers must not import outer layers                     â”‚

â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 2: KEEP ENTITIES PURE                                     â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                      â”‚

â”‚   Business logic only                                           â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 3: USE CASES ARE THE API                                  â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                  â”‚
â”‚   Define application capabilities through use cases             â”‚
â”‚   One use case = one user intention                             â”‚
â”‚   Keep them focused and cohesive                                â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 4: SEPARATE READ AND WRITE MODELS                         â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                        â”‚
â”‚   Consider CQRS for complex domains                             â”‚

â”‚   Commands go through full architecture                         â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 5: HANDLE CROSS-CUTTING AT BOUNDARIES                     â”‚

â”‚   Logging, authentication at interface adapter layer            â”‚

â”‚   Keep core clean of infrastructure concerns                    â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 6: DON'T OVER-ABSTRACT                                    â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                      â”‚

â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 7: PRAGMATIC MAPPING                                      â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                      â”‚

â”‚                                                                 â”‚

3.11 CQRS (Command Query Responsibility Segregation)

Pattern Overview

â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚                         CQRS PATTERN                             â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   ORIGIN: Greg Young, based on CQS by Bertrand Meyer            â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   DEFINITION:                                                   â”‚
â”‚   A pattern that separates read and write operations into       â”‚
â”‚   different models, potentially using different data stores     â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   CQS vs CQRS:                                                  â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                                  â”‚
â”‚   CQS (Command Query Separation):                               â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ Method level - commands vs queries                          â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ Same model for both                                         â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   CQRS (Command Query Responsibility Segregation):              â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ Architecture level - separate models                        â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ Potentially separate data stores                            â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TRADITIONAL (SINGLE MODEL):                                   â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                  â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”   â”‚
â”‚   â”‚                      CLIENT                             â”‚   â”‚
â”‚   â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜   â”‚

â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚              WHEN TO USE EVENT SOURCING                          â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   âœ… EXCELLENT FIT:                                             â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                             â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ Audit trail is a requirement                                â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ Need to know "what happened" not just "what is"             â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ Complex domain with rich business events                    â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ Financial systems (transactions, ledgers)                   â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ Collaborative systems (version history needed)              â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ Need temporal queries                                       â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ Event-driven architecture already in use                    â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   DOMAIN EXAMPLES:                                              â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ Banking (account transactions)                              â”‚

â”‚   â€¢ Inventory (stock movements)                                 â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ Gaming (player actions)                                     â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ Document management (version history)                       â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   âŒ POOR FIT:                                                  â”‚

â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ Simple CRUD applications                                    â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ No audit requirements                                       â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ Team unfamiliar with pattern                                â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ Strong consistency required everywhere                      â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ Lots of updates to same entity (event explosion)            â”‚
â”‚   â€¢ Simple reporting needs                                      â”‚

Event Sourcing Optimization Tips

â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚              EVENT SOURCING OPTIMIZATION TIPS                    â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 1: USE SNAPSHOTS WISELY                                   â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                   â”‚
â”‚   Create snapshots after N events (e.g., every 100)             â”‚
â”‚   Don't snapshot too frequently (storage cost)                  â”‚
â”‚   Snapshot format should be versioned                           â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 2: DESIGN EVENTS CAREFULLY                                â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                â”‚
â”‚   Events are immutable - think before you publish               â”‚
â”‚   Include enough context to be self-contained                   â”‚
â”‚   Use past tense (OrderPlaced, not PlaceOrder)                  â”‚
â”‚   Version your events from the start                            â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 3: PLAN FOR EVENT EVOLUTION                               â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                               â”‚
â”‚   Events will change over time                                  â”‚
â”‚   Use upcasting to transform old events                         â”‚
â”‚   Maintain backward compatibility                               â”‚
â”‚   Consider event schema registry                                â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 4: COMBINE WITH CQRS                                      â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                  â”‚
â”‚   Event Sourcing + CQRS is natural fit                          â”‚
â”‚   Events feed read model projections                            â”‚
â”‚   Separate write (events) from read (projections)               â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 5: KEEP STREAMS SHORT                                     â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                     â”‚
â”‚   Avoid aggregates with millions of events                      â”‚
â”‚   Consider closing and opening new streams                      â”‚
â”‚   Archive old events if needed                                  â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 6: IDEMPOTENT EVENT HANDLERS                              â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                              â”‚
â”‚   Projections may process events multiple times                 â”‚
â”‚   Make handlers idempotent                                      â”‚
â”‚   Track last processed event                                    â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 7: HANDLE PROJECTION REBUILD                              â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                              â”‚
â”‚   You WILL need to rebuild projections                          â”‚
â”‚   Plan for this from the start                                  â”‚
â”‚   Maintain ability to replay all events                         â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 8: CONSIDER EVENT STORE SELECTION                         â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                        â”‚
â”‚   Purpose-built: EventStoreDB, Axon                             â”‚
â”‚   General: PostgreSQL, MongoDB with careful design              â”‚
â”‚   Streaming: Kafka (with caveats)                               â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   TIP 9: DON'T PUT EVERYTHING IN EVENTS                         â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                         â”‚
â”‚   Not all data needs event sourcing                             â”‚
â”‚   Reference data can be traditionally stored                    â”‚
â”‚   Event source the core domain only                             â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜

3.14 Section Summary and Key Takeaways

â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚               KEY TAKEAWAYS - SECTION 3                          â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   STRUCTURAL PATTERNS:                                          â”‚
â”‚   âœ… Monolith: Start here, simple and effective for most cases  â”‚
â”‚   âœ… Modular Monolith: Best of both worlds, clear boundaries    â”‚
â”‚   âœ… Layered: Standard approach, good for separation            â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   DISTRIBUTED PATTERNS:                                         â”‚
â”‚   âœ… Microservices: For scale and team autonomy, high cost      â”‚
â”‚   âœ… SOA: Enterprise integration, often legacy                  â”‚
â”‚   âœ… Serverless: Variable load, operational simplicity          â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   COMMUNICATION PATTERNS:                                       â”‚
â”‚   âœ… Event-Driven: Loose coupling, async, scalable              â”‚
â”‚   âœ… Choreography vs Orchestration: Decentralized vs central    â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   STRUCTURAL ORGANIZATION:                                      â”‚
â”‚   âœ… Hexagonal: Ports & Adapters for testability                â”‚
â”‚   âœ… Clean Architecture: Dependency rule, framework-free core   â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   DATA PATTERNS:                                                â”‚
â”‚   âœ… CQRS: Separate read/write when patterns differ             â”‚
â”‚   âœ… Event Sourcing: Full history, audit, temporal queries      â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   CRITICAL INSIGHTS:                                            â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                             â”‚
â”‚   âš ï¸ No pattern is universally best - context is everything     â”‚
â”‚   âš ï¸ Start simple, add complexity only when needed              â”‚
â”‚   âš ï¸ Patterns can and should be combined                        â”‚
â”‚   âš ï¸ Consider team skills and organizational structure          â”‚
â”‚   âš ï¸ The cost of distributed systems is often underestimated    â”‚
â”‚   âš ï¸ You can always evolve - don't over-engineer upfront        â”‚
â”‚                                                                 â”‚
â”‚   PATTERN SELECTION RULES:                                      â”‚
â”‚   â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€                                     â”‚
â”‚   1. Start with the simplest pattern that could work            â”‚
â”‚   2. Add complexity when you feel pain                          â”‚
â”‚   3. Match pattern to team capabilities                         â”‚
â”‚   4. Consider total cost of ownership                           â”‚
â”‚   5. Plan for evolution, don't plan for perfection              â”‚
